arms race** to guarantee their own security]{.underline}. In short: Bust
the Baltic, and you bust the West. The way to stop that is to strengthen
the NBP9.

### Frontline States\-\--U\-\--2NC

#### Now is key. Russia is [testing]{.underline} NATO resolve.

**Musa '25** \[Miriam; July 12; journalist; Rolling out, "NATO just made
a demand that has allies worried,"
https://rollingout.com/2025/07/12/nato-surprising-move-stuns-europe/;
Accessed 7.18.25\]

Alliance unity under pressure

[The **escalation** tests NATO's unity as member countries face
**difficult** **decisions** about escalation risks versus the need for
**stronger deterrence**]{.underline}. Some members advocate for
immediate deployment of advanced systems, while others worry about
provoking further Russian aggression.

[Eastern **European members**]{.underline}, particularly those bordering
Ukraine, [are pushing hardest for **immediate action** and **enhanced
protection**]{.underline}. They argue that waiting for further
escalation could prove catastrophic for regional security.

Western European allies, while supportive, are weighing [the risks of
escalation against the imperative to protect **alliance territory** and
maintain **credible** **deterrence**]{.underline}.

## Impact\-\--Germany

### 1NC

#### Eroding U.S. deterrence spurs German proliferation.

**Kuhn '24** \[Dr. Ulruch; director of the Arms Control and Emerging
Technologies Program at the University of Hamburg, nonresident scholar
with the Nuclear Policy Program of the Carnegie Endowment for
International Peace; Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists, "Germany debates
nuclear weapons, again. But now it's different,"
https://thebulletin.org/2024/03/germany-debates-nuclear-weapons-again-but-now-its-different/;
Accessed 7.16.25\]

\<\<Title\>\> Germany debates nuclear weapons, again. [But **now it's
different**]{.underline}

[What's **different** this time]{.underline}? The latest iteration of
the German nuclear debate nevertheless shows some key differences from
previous ones. [First, it takes place in a European security environment
that has moved much closer to the scenario of **US abandonment** and
**Russian aggr**ession]{.underline} than most assumed back in 2016, when
Trump rattled Europeans for the first time. As a consequence,
[**proliferation chatter** is not an exclusively **German specialty**
anymore]{.underline}. Most notably Polish leaders, including President
Andrzej Duda and new Foreign Minister Radoslaw Sikorski, have publicly
mused about nuclear weapons other than the United States'.

Second, while the early German nuclear debates featured mostly pundits,
journalists, and some political backbenchers, [those who now favorably
discuss deterrence alternatives increasingly include current and former
**heavyweights** from across the **political spectrum**]{.underline}.
They include Friedrich Merz, Wolfgang Schäuble, and Manfred Weber from
the Conservatives, Sigmar Gabriel and Katarina Barley from the Social
Democrats, and Joschka Fischer and Sergey Logodinsky from the Greens.
When Germany's Finance Minister Christian Lindner from the Free
Democrats joined the chorus in mid-February, Chancellor Olaf Scholz
finally had to put his foot down: He reminded his fellow coalition
partner that "Germany decided a long time ago not to seek its own
nuclear weapons."

Third, [nuclear disarmament]{.underline}---a central pillar of post-Cold
War German foreign and security policy---[does not play a role in the
German **public discourse** any more]{.underline}. When in March 2022
Annalena Baerbock, Germany's Foreign Minister from the Greens, urged
Germans in response to Russia's aggression against Ukraine to
"understand disarmament and arms control as being complementary to
deterrence and defense," everyone in Berlin got the point. [A recent
comparative analysis of Bundestag statements found that the
word]{.underline} "**[disarmament]{.underline}**" [barely showed up in
parliamentary debates in **2022**]{.underline}---[a stark
**difference**]{.underline} with previous years. [Prior iterations of
the German nuclear debate had seen multiple expert interventions in
favor of **disarmament**]{.underline} and arms control policies. But
[these voices have mostly **gone silent** now]{.underline}.

Fourth, [a newfound **hawkishness** has come to **dominate** the German
media discourse]{.underline}. [Fueled by a few dozen hardline **think
tankers**]{.underline} and politicians, [restraint]{.underline}---in
every form, including the obvious limitations of a mutual deterrence
relationship with Russia---[is considered **weak** and a sign of
**fear** of **Russia**]{.underline}. "Self-deterrence" is the main
charge levelled against Scholz to dismiss every consideration of
potential escalation pathways vis-à-vis Russia.

[All this happens on the back of a shift in **public
opinion**]{.underline}. Latest surveys show that Germans see nuclear
weapons much less negatively than in the past. In a poll conducted by
German pollster Infratest-dimap in mid 2022, for the first time in
decades a majority of respondents said they welcomed US nuclear weapons
deployed on German soil. When the German nuclear debate kicked off in
2016, nuclear skeptics could still claim that the entire discussion was
out of touch with Germans' long-standing preference for nuclear
abolition. Today, that is no longer a clear-cut case.

What's next? So far in the debate, the shifting parameters have not gone
so far as to lead the government to pursue any visible changes to
Germany's deterrence arrangements. No less important, 90 percent of
Germans reject the notion that the country should have its own nuclear
weapons. [The combination of Germany's **security dependence** and
**political conservatism**]{.underline}, **[however]{.underline}**,
[might lead to **difficult choices** ahead]{.underline}.

[A reelection of Trump and subsequent **policy changes** in **US nuclear
guarantees** to **European allies** could lay bare the **obvious
downsides** of German **dependency**]{.underline}. At the same time,
[German conservatism could force the country to search for **deterrence
alternatives**]{.underline} in such a scenario.

#### \<\<German Prolif Impact\>\>

### Germany\-\--Impact\-\--2NC

#### European proliferation causes [miscalc]{.underline} and domino effects.

Liviu **Horovitz &** Claudia **Major 6-17**, Associate in the
International Security Research Group at the German Institute for
International and Security Affairs, Ph.D. in Comparative and
International Studies from ETH Zürich; Senior Vice President for
Transatlantic Security at the German Marshall Fund of the United States,
Ph.D. from the University of Birmingham, "Should Europeans develop an
independent nuclear deterrent?", Brookings, 06-17-2025, accessed
07-16-2025,
https://www.brookings.edu/articles/should-europeans-develop-an-independent-nuclear-deterrent,
DG

[The unintended consequences of a broadly proliferated landscape
are]{.underline} even more [**significant**. Should]{.underline} the
[principal European nations]{.underline} choose to [develop nuclear
arsenals, numerous secondary powers would]{.underline} likely [**pursue
the same path**, culminating in a **precarious, nuclear-armed Europe**
marked by volatile internal and external relations]{.underline}. While
these [nations]{.underline} might feel more secure vis-à-vis Russia,
many [would]{.underline} simultaneously [fear both **deliberate** and
**accidental escalation**, and harbor deep suspicions about their
neighbors' intentions]{.underline}---[especially in an environment
characterized by **political polarization** and the **growing influence
of extremist forces**]{.underline} across Europe. [The pursuit of
greater security would paradoxically result in a **less secure overall
environment**]{.underline}.

#### It goes global.

Stephen J. **Cimbala &** Lawrence J. **Korb 24**, Distinguished
Professor of Political Science at Penn State Bradywine, Ph.D. in
Political Science from the University of Wisconsin-Madison; Senior
Fellow at the Center for American Progress, Adjunct Professor of
Security Studies at Georgetown University, "Even in the face of Russian
aggression, a nuclear 'Eurodeterrent' is still a bad idea," Bulletin of
the Atomic Scientists, 02-12-2024,
https://thebulletin.org/2024/02/even-in-the-face-of-russian-aggression-a-nuclear-eurodeterrent-is-still-a-bad-idea,
DG

[Policy makers]{.underline} and national security experts
[are]{.underline} now **[seriously discussing]{.underline}** the
possible need for [a European nuclear deterrent]{.underline}. For
example, former German Foreign Minister Joschka Fischer, in an interview
with the weekly Die Zeit in December 2023, supported the idea of a
European nuclear deterrent. Other supporters of a European---or even a
German---nuclear arsenal have contributed regularly to conservative
German newspapers.

In 1975, the West German government stated that its ratification of the
accession of the Federal Republic to the Nuclear Nonproliferation Treaty
would not preclude an eventual nuclear deterrent within the framework of
the European Union. The issue of a European nuclear deterrent also
blends into the related question as to what role, if any, remains for
so-called "tactical" or non-strategic nuclear weapons in NATO.\[1\]

There are several political and military arguments in favor of this
proposal. But there are certainly also reasons for skepticism and
concern that, considered together, add up to the conclusion that [a
Eurodeterrent **continues to be a bad idea**]{.underline}.\[2\]

Rationale for a Eurodeterrent. The first reason given for a European
nuclear deterrent is the possibility that a future US president might
not be as committed to NATO as his or her predecessors. This was a
concern raised during the administration of President Donald Trump,
whose open disparagement of European allies for allegedly insufficient
financial contributions to NATO alliance security raised alarms from
Brussels to Washington. Although [Trump's actual policies turned
out]{.underline} to be [less disruptive than his]{.underline}
inflammatory [rhetoric]{.underline}, his possible return to the White
House in 2025 could reboot European doubts about US security
commitments. These doubts could include concerns about the role of
America's nuclear umbrella in the deterrence of war in Europe.

A second reason for the renewed interest in a possible European nuclear
deterrent is the war in Ukraine and the recurring threats by Russian
President Vladimir Putin to resort to nuclear first use if needed to
protect Russian security interests in that region.\[3\] [Some
Europeans]{.underline} who [feel immediately threatened by Russian
nuclear coercion]{.underline}, or even by the possibility of a Russian
conventional military victory over Ukraine, might doubt the efficacy of
America's nuclear guarantee in the face of an increasingly confident
Russia [casting a larger shadow over]{.underline} the security of
**[Eastern and Central European countries]{.underline}**. Although
Russia would still have its hands full of resistance and rebellion in
Ukraine, even after having defenestrated the Zelensky government,
European and American solidarity to further resist Russian diplomatic
coercion supported by military power might weaken.

A third reason might be the growing nationalism in Europe and in the
United States, spanning across the general public, leadership classes,
and political office holders. The heady optimism about the North
Atlantic security community that marked the Cold War and its immediate
aftermath is now being challenged from many sides. Newer generations of
voters show skepticism about the benefits of transnational and
trans-Atlantic security and economic cooperation. There is also the
growing significance among Europeans and Americans of identity politics,
including doubts about the impact of foreign migration and so-called
alarms over a dilution of national character. Parties of the far right
are drawing increased strength in part from voters who feel
denationalized by foreign influences and globalist agendas.

[A fourth concern]{.underline} among some supporters of a European
nuclear deterrent [lies in **doubts about the reliability of the US
nuclear umbrella**]{.underline} for the deterrence of war in Europe.
NATO has only a small number of deployed nuclear weapons on European
soil compared to Russia's large inventory of nearly two thousand
less-than-strategic or "tactical" nuclear weapons. Russia might be
tempted under pessimistic conditions of unacceptable loss in
conventional warfare, as they define "unacceptable," to cross the
nuclear threshold in any one of a number of ways.

Russia could engage in a demonstrative strike for coercive purposes with
a low-yield nuclear weapon, which would do little immediate damage
compared to the larger weapons that are prepositioned in its arsenal and
raised to combat alert readiness in a crisis. Or Russia could employ a
tactical nuclear weapon against a key Ukrainian or NATO military target
to prepare the battlespace for a larger war. Would the United States and
NATO risk responding with nuclear strikes of their own and thereby
escalating the conflict to a larger nuclear war that might destroy much
of Europe and ultimately the US homeland? Some military experts feel
that the United States lacks a sufficient number of small yield nuclear
weapons in its arsenal, compared to Russia's larger inventories, and so
would have lost escalation dominance across the spectrum of military
options short of unrestricted nuclear warfare.

But [more weapons do not]{.underline} necessarily [equate to better
deterrence]{.underline}. Deterrence remains centered in the mind of the
deterree, not the deterrer. [Guessing the threshold of conventional
defeat beyond which Russia would]{.underline} feel obligated to [resort
to nuclear first use requires disciplined intelligence
collection]{.underline} in real time, combined with mature judgment
about Russia's imminent priorities and risks.

Why it's a bad idea. Despite these legitimate concerns about nuclear
risks in Europe, it is not clear whether a European nuclear
deterrent---either entirely separated from its American umbrella or
somehow aligned with it for policy purposes---would enhance the
credibility of deterrence or nuclear crisis stability. In fact, at least
for four reasons, the opposite result is more probable.

First, [legitimate concern should not give way to exaggerated fears
of]{.underline} NATO or [US abandonment]{.underline} of their political
commitment to the deterrence of war in Europe or its defense if
necessary. One might argue that NATO was more likely to wobble in the
face of Russian intimidation when NATO was preoccupied with "out of the
area" commitments in the Middle East and South Asia. Since 2014,
however, when Russia annexed Crimea in a coup de main that caught the
West asleep, [NATO has witnessed a **renaissance of awareness** about
its]{.underline} original [mission to guarantee the peace in
Europe]{.underline}. Russia's war against [Ukraine]{.underline} further
[aroused NATO to a **unified** political and military
**stance**]{.underline}. However the conventional war between Russia and
Ukraine turns out, NATO is stronger than it has ever been since the Cold
War ended. The addition of Finland and the imminent addition of Sweden
to NATO membership represent geostrategic fiascoes for Russia---and an
outcome Putin and his advisors surely had not anticipated. NATO's
nuclear guarantee, [supported by the **U**nited **S**tates' nuclear
umbrella]{.underline}, should never be doubted. Escalation is a two way
street: [Russia fears as much as European NATO countries that a
**nuclear first use** could lead to a process that
**escapes**]{.underline} the **[control]{.underline}** of political
leaderships playing at nuclear coercive diplomacy under unprecedented
conditions of stress and uncertainty.

A second challenge for a European nuclear deterrent lies in its
organization and operational shape, including the chain of command over
a (presumably) multinational nuclear force. Something like this was
attempted in the 1960s when several military planners and politicians
put forward a proposal for a Multilateral Force (MLF) mixing personnel
and weapons, including nuclear warheads, from several European powers.
[The problems of authoritative political control and operational
management]{.underline} in such a force soon [became
**evident**]{.underline} and proved to be too challenging. Even short of
an explicitly multinational force, a transnational European nuclear
deterrent presents problems of political collaboration and
military-operational command. One might propose that the United Kingdom
and France, currently the only two nuclear weapons states among European
NATO countries, move toward joint operations with respect to certain
well defined contingencies and emergencies. Yet their political and
military command and control systems would still have to remain separate
for reasons of national sovereignty and military efficiency. In
addition, because the United Kingdom is no longer in the European Union,
a "Eurodeterrent" would not be under the political supervision of EU
institutions and therefore be reduced to a bilateral cooperation.
Another issue is that the European nuclear powers have very different
delivery systems for nuclear weapons: The United Kingdom fields a fleet
of ballistic missile submarines, while France also has land-based and
carrier-based airborne delivery systems.

A third riposte to the idea of [a European nuclear
deterrent]{.underline} is that it [might **stimulate nuclear
prolif**eration and]{.underline} ultimately [lead to **more nuclear**
weapons **states**]{.underline}, either [**in Europe or beyond**.
Reliance on the US nuclear guarantee]{.underline} has
[helped]{.underline} to [prevent the spread of **nuc**lear weapon**s**
not only in Europe, but also in **Asia** with respect to South **Korea
and Japan**. If countries]{.underline} now [start doubting the
**cred**ibility of the US nuclear deterrent and feel immediately
threatened]{.underline} by the growing capabilities of hostile
neighbors, [their leaders and publics]{.underline} might **[turn to
national nuclear forces]{.underline}** as a guarantor against aggression
or nuclear coercion. This could be tempting because, based on the
historical evidence, even a small number of nuclear weapons can be
deterring to adversaries who might otherwise contemplate a conventional
war against a state. If North Korea continues to menace South Korea with
military threats, including nuclear ones, [South Korea might
**reverse**]{.underline} its **[prior disinterest]{.underline}** in its
own nuclear force. [Japan]{.underline} too, if it feels [threatened by a
rising China, might reluctantly become more interested]{.underline} in a
small but survivable force of sea-based nuclear missiles.

Fourth, [the argument]{.underline} that [the US nuclear force is
insufficiently flexible]{.underline} for deterring or responding to
nuclear coercion or nuclear first use [deserves]{.underline} further
[scrutiny]{.underline}. The United States' combatant commanders who
operate the unified and specified commands in wartime are also
responsible for planning against a range of threats across the spectrum
of conventional war and nuclear deterrence. [Flexibility and multiple
options are built into]{.underline} these [plans for **every
challenge**, ranging from unconventional wars and coercive diplomacy to
limited or large-scale conventional war to]{.underline}, if necessary,
**[nuclear]{.underline}** coercion or **[retaliation]{.underline}**.
Plans are scrubbed repeatedly and road-tested in near-realistic
exercises.

In today's world of high tech competition among major powers, [neither
the **U**nited **S**tates nor any other aspiring military can afford to
rest on dated plans]{.underline}, aging technology, or roseate
assumptions about the likelihood of wars. Surprises are inevitable:
preparation and agility are the hallmarks of superior military
performance. As Dwight D. Eisenhower famously said: "Plans are
worthless, but planning is everything."

#### Nuclear proliferation causes nuclear war. 

**Wolfstahl et al. 25**, Jon Wolfstahl, Director of Global Risk at the
Federation of American Scientists, former Special Assistant to President
of the United States Barack Obama for National Security Affairs; Hans
Kristensen, Director of the Nuclear Information Project at the
Federation of American Scientists; Matt Korda, Associate Director for
the Nuclear Information Project at the Federation of American
Scientists, "What's Making Some Countries Daydream About Nukes Again?",
The Washington Post, 06-29-2025, accessed 07-16-2025, ProQuest, DG

[A world filled with nuclear states is]{.underline} a **[more
dangerous]{.underline}** world. The United States and Soviet Union had
multiple close calls [during the Cold War: mistakes, escalation and
technical glitches]{.underline}. We know that [a world with many more
nuclear states will be plagued in the same way. **Any small clash could
risk a wider nuclear conflict**. It is a matter of **hard work and
luck**]{.underline} that [it hasn\'t happened already]{.underline}. Will
the same hold true for the new nuclear-armed powers? One need look no
farther than India and Pakistan to see that having nuclear weapons has
neither kept the peace nor prevented escalation on a regular basis. The
attacks and counterattacks in May reminded the world that [escalation
remains a **constant risk** when nuclear states clash. With nuclear
weapons, **leaders have to get every decision right all the time. The
more countries that have nuclear weapons, the more our luck will be
tested**]{.underline}.

The chain of [global nuclear security is only as good as its weakest
link]{.underline}. The United States has worked for many decades to help
nations protect civilian research facilities with sensitive nuclear
materials to avoid enriched uranium or plutonium from falling into the
wrong hands. But [more nuclear states will mean more **targets for
theft** by bad actors]{.underline} - **[state and
non-state]{.underline}** - [as well as by criminal networks and
disgruntled employees]{.underline}. Even if one country manages its
responsibilities well, [a sudden change in government, coup or economic
collapse can quickly turn a nuclear arsenal into an acute
problem]{.underline}.

#### Extinction

Stephen **Markley 16**, Journalist at Paste Magazine, "Is France the
Final Domino for the European Union?", Paste Magazine, 12-15-2016,
accessed 07-14-2025,
https://www.pastemagazine.com/articles/2016/12/is-france-the-final-domino-for-the-european-union.html,

For many Americans reading this, there is no doubt a sense of remove
from the relevance of the European project. Think of it this way: [for
centuries Europe was the Middle East of the world. It was a war-hungry,
dysfunctional, violent, chaotic piece of the globe that could barely go
a decade without a barbaric, usually pointless blood-letting conflict.
Since World War II]{.underline} and the **[economic and political
integration]{.underline}** that [brought]{.underline} former
nation-state rivals into mutually beneficial [coexistence]{.underline},
the continent has been a region of [remarkable prosperity and
stability]{.underline}. Keep in mind, this has never happened before in
human history: a set of political leaders and their constituents
voluntarily chose to cede elements of sovereignty in order to form a
better, stronger union. The United States and Great Britain, often
mentioned as democratic ideals, were two empires won by violent conquest
in which wealthy elites only begrudgingly allowed democracy to sift down
to the most impoverished and brutalized citizens.

The parochial movements of the last five to ten years---from the EU's
troubles to the Scottish independence vote to the surge of American
nationalism that brought us such phenomenon as the go-it-alone war in
Iraq and now Donald Trump---have different roots and causes but they do
share a common thread. [We live in an increasingly complex and
interdependent world where the greatest threats to peace, individual
well-being, and human flourishing are largely **transnational**.
**Climate change**, **infectious disease**, **nuclear proliferation**,
**financial crisis**, **cyber insecurity**]{.underline}---these [are all
borderless phenomenon]{.underline} that do not care if their victims are
French, British, American, or Syrian. Their worst consequences do not
care about our flags, languages, religions, or skin colors. And [yet at
a time when transnational peril necessitates a greater need for
cooperation, tolerance, and unity, Western liberal democracies are
taking a hard turn into fragmentation, arrogance, self-pity, and denial.
The break-up of the **E**]{.underline}uropean **[U]{.underline}**nion
brought on by a wave of selfish, quasi-authoritarian nationalism [would
be a **staggering blow** to the economic and political security of what
remains of the increasingly precarious free **world**]{.underline}.

### Germany\-\--Impact\-\--Russia\-\--2NC

#### Russia strikes first.

Andrea **Gilli &** Francesco **Nicoli 25**, Senior Researcher in
Military Affairs at the NATO Defense College, Ph.D. in Social and
Political Science from the European University Institute; Assistant
Professor of Political Economy at the Turin Institute of Technology,
Ph.D. from the University of Trento, "How Can Europe's Nuclear
Deterrence Trilemma Be Resolved?", Bruegel Working Paper, No. 12/2025,
2025, accessed 07-18-2025,
https://www.bruegel.org/sites/default/files/2025-06/WP%2012%202025_1.pdf,
DG

The second, evident implication of the withdrawal of US nuclear
deterrence from Europe would be the risk of nuclear proliferation. The
[US's extended nuclear deterrence]{.underline} has, over the years,
explicitly [aimed to counter proliferation]{.underline}. Meanwhile, [US
allies have used the 'threat' of proliferation to bargain]{.underline}
for more extensive nuclear guarantees (Volpe, 2017; Lutsch, 2022; Jang,
2016; Bowers and Hiim, 2021). However, if we accept that nuclear weapons
represent an effective deterrence, [the]{.underline} abrupt
[disappearance of the US nuclear deterrent]{.underline} from Europe [may
**push countries to pursue their own nuclear weapons**.
Poland]{.underline}, for instance, [has been hinting at nuclear
ambitions]{.underline} since 2022 to counter Russia if NATO falters and,
more recently, has reiterated these plans4. [Ukraine]{.underline},
stripped of its Soviet- era nuclear weapons in 1994 under the Budapest
Memorandum, has similarly [voiced regret and interest in
rearmament]{.underline}, citing Russia's violation of security
guarantees5.

[Proliferation]{.underline}, however, [can promote **crisis
instability** and **arms races**]{.underline}. From a rational
perspective, [countries have]{.underline}, in fact, [an incentive to
**neutralise threats before they grow more acute**, and]{.underline}
thus [proliferation may **invite enemy attacks**]{.underline} (Fearon,
1995). For instance, [if Russia were faced with diffused nuclear
rearmament]{.underline} across Eastern Europe, [it might decide to
**intervene militarily by striking the development sites**, similarly to
what has happened against Iraq and Syria]{.underline}, and to what the
US and Israel have considered in relation to Iran (Raas and Long, 2007).
[This]{.underline}, in turn, [can invite other destabilising
actions]{.underline}, ultimately **[fuelling greater crisis
instability]{.underline}** (Bas and Coe, 2018).

### Germany\-\--Impact\-\--AT: Impact Defense

#### German prolif causes [Russia war]{.underline}, unravels [global nonproliferation efforts]{.underline}, and destroys the [EU]{.underline}.

Wolfgang **Ischinger 18**, Professor of Security Policy and Diplomatic
Practice at the Hertie School of Governance, Chairman of the Munich
Security Conference, former German Ambassador to the United States,
"Germany's Dangerous Nuclear Flirtation," Project Syndicate, 08-08-2018,
https://www.project-syndicate.org/commentary/germany-nuclear-weapon-debate-by-wolfgang-ischinger-2018-08

As in a game of chess, there are geopolitical moves through which a
country can -- unwittingly -- checkmate itself. Opening a debate on
German nuclear weapons would be such a move. Yet this is exactly what
some Germans have recently proposed. Supporters of a nuclear-armed
Germany contend that NATO's nuclear umbrella has lost all credibility
because of statements made by US President Donald Trump.

There are at least three good reasons why [considering a nuclear option
would be **foolhardy for Germany**]{.underline}. For starters,
[Germany]{.underline} has [repeatedly renounced it]{.underline}, first
in 1969 [by signing]{.underline} ([and]{.underline} later
[ratifying]{.underline}) [the]{.underline} Treaty on the
Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons ([NPT]{.underline}),
[and]{.underline} then in 1990 by signing [the]{.underline} so-called
[Two Plus Four Treaty]{.underline}, which paved the way for German
reunification. [Casting doubt on these]{.underline} commitments
[would]{.underline} severely [**damage Germany's rep**utation **and
reliability**]{.underline} worldwide. [Germany would call into question
the credibility of **NATO**'s nuclear deterrence, and]{.underline} thus
[**the alliance itself**, along with the **entire nuclear
non-prolif**eration **regime**]{.underline}.

It is worth noting that since its creation in 1949, NATO has been one of
the world's most successful instruments of proliferation prevention. Not
a single NATO member state -- apart from the United States, the United
Kingdom, and France -- has found it necessary to acquire nuclear weapons
of its own. [If Germany were now to break out]{.underline} of its
non-nuclear power status, [what would keep **Turkey** or
**Poland**]{.underline}, for instance, [from **follow**ing
**suit**]{.underline}? Germany as a gravedigger of the international
non-proliferation regime -- who could want that?

Second, [a German nuclear bomb would damage]{.underline} the strategic
environment in [Europe]{.underline} -- to Germany's disadvantage.
[Russia would interpret German steps toward a nuclear arsenal as a
**direct threat**]{.underline} to its own national security [and
would]{.underline} likely [adopt]{.underline} military [countermeasures.
That]{.underline}, in turn, [would make it even harder to
pursue]{.underline} the vision [of a pan-European order of peace and
security]{.underline}, a core foreign-policy goal of all German
governments since that of Konrad Adenauer. Moreover, [a German nuclear
ambition might **jeopardize the delicate balance of power in
Europe**]{.underline} -- including [between Germany and
France]{.underline}, for example -- [with incalculable consequences
for]{.underline} the long-term cohesion of [the **E**uropean
**U**nion]{.underline}.

Finally, it is not hard to predict that the pursuit of nuclear weapons
would draw significant public opposition, especially given that such a
move would be a complete about-face for German Chancellor Angela
Merkel's government, which, just a few years ago, moved to phase out
nuclear energy altogether. [It is difficult to imagine a **greater
fiasco**]{.underline} for German foreign and security policy [than
proposing a nuclear strategy]{.underline} and then failing to obtain
parliamentary approval.

#### Moves towards prolif cause [Russian preemptive strikes]{.underline} AND German miscalc. 

Ulrich **Kühn &** Tristan **Volpe 17**, Fellow in the Nuclear Policy
Program at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, Ph.D. in

https://www.foreignaffairs.com/germany/keine-atombombe-bitte

Worst of all, [the pursuit of a German nuclear arsenal]{.underline},
rather than deterring aggression, [could **increase the risk of conflict
in Europe**]{.underline}, since [Russia would]{.underline} likely [work
to prevent Germany from acquiring the bomb. Moscow could assassinate
German]{.underline} nuclear [scientists, use
**cyberattacks**]{.underline} to sabotage German nuclear industrial
infrastructure, [and]{.underline} perhaps go so far as to [**strike
German nuclear facilities from the air**. Even covert

confrontation**]{.underline}.

[Even if Germany managed to acquire **nuc**lear weapon**s**, it would
then face the daunting task of making sure they could survive a Russian

westward, targeting Germany and other NATO members. Now that [Russia

missiles]{.underline}** in violation of the 1987 Intermediate-Range

#### All medium-sized nations would proliferate.

Andreas **Rüesch 24**, Journalist and Editor in the Foreign Editorial
Office at Neue Zürcher Zeitung, "The last thing Europe needs is a

A second argument concerns hurdles under international law. With the

([NPT]{.underline}). [And in the **Two Plus Four Treaty**]{.underline}

would]{.underline} therefore [be seen]{.underline} in Moscow [as a

#### Nuclear sharing destroys the EU.

Tom **Sauer 20**, Associate Professor of International Politics at the
University of Antwerp, Ph.D. in International Politics from KU Leuven,
"Power and Nuclear Weapons: The Case of the European Union," Journal for
Peace and Nuclear Disarmament, Vol. 3, No. 1, 2020,
https://doi.org/10.1080/25751654.2020.1764260

There are two reasons why the issue of the Eurobomb is only sporadically
touched upon: first of all, many observers of European security,
especially in the US, the UK and Eastern Europe, do not believe in
further defense integration in the EU, let alone a European army, and
for that reason perceive a Euro-bomb as unrealistic. According to these
voices, [Europe continues to rely on NATO, including]{.underline} on
[the US nuclear umbrella]{.underline} (Rühle and Rühle 2017). Secondly,
[apart from France, there is a **strong anti-nuclear tendency in Western
Europe**, certainly amongst the general public. Even if a European army
is seen as a preferable goal]{.underline} by many Western European
citizens, [that does not mean]{.underline} that [they are]{.underline}
also [in favor of Europeanizing the French nuclear weapons]{.underline}.
That is also the reason why some predict that [if the Eurobomb is
realized, it will be done in secret]{.underline} (Fischer 2017a;
Tertrais 2019, 61). [The latter]{.underline}, however, [may
**undermine** the **legitimacy** of European defense integration **and
the E**uropean **U**nion project **as such**]{.underline}.

states]{.underline}. Furthermore, [the rejection of the

#### Merz [maintains]{.underline} the Alliance.

**Mead '25** \[Walter Russel; Updated July 14; Distinguished Fellow in
Strategy and Statesmanship at Hudson Institute, Professor of Strategy

University of Florida; WSJ, "Friedrich Merz, Our Man in Berlin,"

Accessed 7.18.25\]

[Mr. Merz]{.underline}, [a **successful** **businessman**]{.underline},
[may have an **easier time** understanding the **American president**
than do career politicians with less feel for the private
sector]{.underline}. [Knowing that a **working relationship** with Mr.
Trump was a **key** to his **success**]{.underline} overall, [the new
German chancellor has tended the bilateral relationship
**assiduously**]{.underline}. At his first Oval Office meeting, Mr. Merz
presented the president with a gold-framed copy of Mr. Trump's
grandfather's German birth certificate. [Mr. Merz has **refused to
criticize** Mr. **Trump** in public]{.underline} and has nothing but
nice things to say about their telephone conversations.

Bucking a tide of anti-Israel and anti-American sentiment in Europe,
[Mr. Merz was extremely supportive both of **Israel's w**ar against
**Iran** and of **Trump's backing** for the Jewish state]{.underline}.
He praised Israel's strikes against Iran, calling them "dirty work that
Israel is doing for all of us." Mr. Merz was clear about Iran's
intentions: "The evidence that Iran is continuing on its path to
building a nuclear weapon can no longer be seriously disputed." And his
reaction to Mr. Trump's decision to join Israeli military action in Iran
was equally unambiguous: "There is no reason for us, or for me
personally, to criticize what Israel started a week ago, nor is there
any reason to criticize what America did last weekend."

[Mr. Merz's style reflects a return to the conservative and **pragmatic
approach** that made West Germany **America's most reliable
ally**]{.underline} in Continental Europe during the early decades of
the Cold War. Conservative chancellors like Konrad Adenauer and Ludwig
Erhard supported the North Atlantic Treaty Organization and the
trans-Atlantic alliance through thick and thin. France might dream
Gaullist dreams of independence and autonomy, but with East Germany
under Moscow's control, German conservatives believed the American
alliance was their one hope of safety.

[Mr. Merz seems to see the world in **similar terms**]{.underline}.
Vladimir Putin may not pose the same threat to Germany that Joseph
Stalin did when the Red Army camped in the ruins of Berlin, but Mr.
[Putin's growing power and evident hostility mean that **America**
**matters** more for **German security**]{.underline} than it has since
the end of the Cold War. [Mr. Trump has made clear that **America's
continuing engagement** in Europe can't be taken **for
granted**]{.underline}. [Mr. Merz **understands** that and is **acting
accordingly**]{.underline}.

There was another reason German leaders supported the American alliance
in the early Cold War. After Germany launched the two most brutal wars
in European history in 1914 and 1939, its neighbors understandably
didn't trust it. Many in Britain and France opposed German rearmament
during the 1950s but were reassured by the continuing presence of the
Americans.

[The American alliance]{.underline} did more than keep the Russians out
of Europe. It [brought the **Germans** **in**]{.underline}. West Germany
was able to regain the trust of its neighbors and build the cooperative
relationships that promoted German prosperity and, eventually, German
and European unity.

That history could become relevant again. [A Germany that **modernizes**
its **military** and seriously commits to putting 5% of GDP toward
defense would soon have]{.underline}, by far, [the strongest military in
the **E**uropean **U**nion]{.underline}. A continued American engagement
in European security will again reassure Germany's neighbors that they
don't have to worry about an overmighty Berlin.

In any case, [Friedrich Merz appears to understand both the
**importance** and the fragility of the **American** **alliance** better
than his recent predecessors did]{.underline}. There are tests ahead,
but [Mr. Merz's hard-headed, **pragmatic conservatism** may be just what
the trans-Atlantic **alliance** **needs** at a difficult
time]{.underline}.
