### No Link\-\--2AC

#### Europe will [remain reassured]{.underline} despite the plan. 

Liviu **Horovitz &** Claudia **Major 6-17**, Associate in the
International Security Research Group at the German Institute for
International and Security Affairs, Ph.D. in Comparative and
International Studies from ETH Zürich; Senior Vice President for
Transatlantic Security at the German Marshall Fund of the United States,
Ph.D. from the University of Birmingham, "Should Europeans develop an
independent nuclear deterrent?", Brookings, 06-17-2025, accessed
07-16-2025,
https://www.brookings.edu/articles/should-europeans-develop-an-independent-nuclear-deterrent,
DG

Both in public and private, Trump and his key political appointees have
clearly stated that Europe is not a priority, expressed disdain for
Europe's political order, and raised doubts about their commitment to
the continent's security. However, [members of the Trump administration
have repeatedly signaled to the Europeans]{.underline} that [the nuclear
dimension **remains unaffected** by other transatlantic
quarrels]{.underline}. In fact, [the administration has offered
**numerous assurances**]{.underline} that [the U.S. nuclear umbrella
would remain in place for the long term]{.underline}. The U.S.
Department of Defense's Interim National Defense Strategic Guidance,
which was leaked to the press in March, stated that the United States
would continue to support Europe with nuclear deterrence of Russia, even
if Washington would count on the Europeans to take on various other
conventional roles.

The administration's [conventional force posture]{.underline} also
[offers]{.underline} some [reason for **cautious optimism**. For many
allies, the credibility of extended nuclear deterrence is tied
to]{.underline} the presence of substantial U.S. [conventional
forces]{.underline} in Europe. The U.S. Department of Defense has
signaled an intention to reassess the U.S. presence on the continent,
and the posture review is still underway. And it is possible that
decisions to reduce the U.S. presence in Europe might be only taken
after the NATO summit, once all key positions in the Pentagon have been
staffed and the posture review has been concluded. Then again,
[indications are]{.underline} that the [Trump]{.underline}
administration [will reduce some presence, such as the additional troops
deployed after 2022, but **not fundamentally alter its conventional
footprint in Europe**]{.underline}---which can be read as [an indicator
of U.S. willingness to continue]{.underline} to provide [extended
deterrence. While the alliance is increasingly transferring
responsibility to Europe]{.underline}---clearly posing difficult
adaptation challenges---**[the core U.S.-led military structure appears
largely intact]{.underline}**.

Of course, a Russian war of aggression with a clear nuclear dimension on
NATO's border is a fundamentally new phenomenon, and some European
allies fear that, combined with the new U.S. administration's disruptive
goals, these new circumstances might fundamentally alter the
transatlantic alliance. However, [the current situation is]{.underline}
actually [**comparable to earlier transatlantic disagreements** that did
not result in the collapse of extended nuclear deterrence. Severe
economic disputes have **often occurred**]{.underline} throughout the
past eight decades. [Political disputes were common]{.underline};
several allies were autocracies for extended periods. [Capitals on both
sides]{.underline} of the Atlantic **[repeatedly colluded with
Moscow]{.underline}**, much to their allies' dismay.

Lastly, [most Europeans believe that, even under the most restrictive
interpretation of Trump's interests, abandoning extended nuclear
deterrence toward the continent would be **inconsistent**
with]{.underline} achieving [the president's goals, including
maintaining a sufficiently stable international economic order
for]{.underline} the administration to achieve [its domestic
aims]{.underline}. It is undeniable that Trump and many of his
associates aim to fundamentally reshape the international system.
However, they do not appear entirely indifferent to domestic
constraints. This assessment is supported by the administration's
retreat from its maximalist positions when confronted with substantial
economic costs. Also, [the Trump administration's current approach to
NATO seems to suggest]{.underline} that [the U.S.]{.underline}
government [remains **committed to its security assurances** and
**recognizes the strategic importance of allies**]{.underline}. While
U.S. officials may believe that limited conventional crises in Europe
could be regional responsibilities, [massive realignments and the chaos
associated with the collapse of nuclear deterrence can hardly be in the
interest of any U.S. administration]{.underline}. Moreover, [Washington
appears determined to limit Beijing's economic and military options. A
stable, cooperative Europe is **essential to**]{.underline} achieving
**[this]{.underline}** goal, while a collapsing transatlantic
relationship could severely limit U.S. options.

### No Link\-\--1AR

#### No link\-\--nothing short of withdrawal can make abandonment fears enough to motivate proliferation or full-blown nuclear sharing. 

Doreen **Horschig &** Sean **Monaghan 24**, Fellow with the Project on
Nuclear Issues at the Center for Strategic and International Studies,
Ph.D. in Security Studies from the University of Central Florida;
Visiting Fellow in the Europe, Russia, and Eurasia Program at the Center
for Strategic and International Studies, "Europe Needs More Conventional
Forces, Not Its Own Nukes," Center for Strategic and International
Studies, 06-21-2024, accessed 07-20-2025,
https://www.csis.org/analysis/europe-needs-more-conventional-forces-not-its-own-nukes,
DG

All this means that [the so-called]{.underline}
"**[Eurobomb]{.underline}**," "Eurodeterrent," or "Euronukes" [debate
is]{.underline}---in the words of Bruno Tertrais---still [a **red
herring**. A European nuclear deterrent is]{.underline} not only
**[unlikely]{.underline}**, it is also unnecessary---for two reasons.

First, [the U.S. nuclear umbrella is **more robust than Trump's comments
make it seem**]{.underline}. In the 1960s the British defense minister
Denis Healey said, "it takes only 5% credibility of American retaliation
to deter the Russians, but 95% to reassure the Europeans." Healey's
Theorem, as it became known, is often invoked around the challenges of
assuring allies about U.S. extended nuclear deterrence commitments. But
it also highlights that **[deterrence rests on
uncertainty]{.underline}**, not certainty. As an Estonian analyst
explained in the Wall Street Journal: "[For Putin, what really matters
is that the other side that]{.underline} opposes him [has nuclear
weapons and is credible about their use in extreme circumstances. As
long as there still is this 1% chance]{.underline} of the nuclear
element, the [deterrence may still hold]{.underline}."

[Despite]{.underline} the former president's [loose
language]{.underline}---[and **even if he took a more cavalier
position**]{.underline} if he returned to office---the [United States'
adversaries can never be certain]{.underline} that [he would rule out
nuclear use in]{.underline} "[extreme circumstances]{.underline}."
Moreover, **[despite his acerbic rhetoric]{.underline}** about NATO
allies during his four years in office, President **[Trump's
policies]{.underline}**---such as the 2018 Nuclear Posture
Review---**[amounted to a strengthening, not weakening, of the U.S.
nuclear enterprise]{.underline}** and extended deterrence. **[Actions
speak louder than words]{.underline}**.

The second reason Europe does not need a nuclear deterrent is because it
already has two. [The **U**nited **K**ingdom and France both extend
their nuclear deterrents to Europe]{.underline}. [The **U**nited
**K**ingdom does so through NATO]{.underline}, officially declaring: "We
would consider using our nuclear weapons only in extreme circumstances
of self-defence, including the defence of our NATO allies." While
[France]{.underline} does not participate in NATO's nuclear planning or
declare its deterrent to NATO, it [has made clear that its nuclear
arsenal is at the service of European security]{.underline}. As
President Macron stated in 2020 (and reiterated in April): "Let's be
clear: France's vital interests now have a European dimension." (Macron
followed the example of de Gaulle and Pompidou, who made a similar
declaration in 1964, and several leaders since.) Moreover, since 1995
France and the United Kingdom have treated their vital interests as
concomitant.

[The British and French nuclear arsenals]{.underline} are much smaller
than that of the United States---consisting of a few hundred warheads
rather than a few thousand---but [are large enough to inflict
unacceptable damage. These two]{.underline} European arsenals [enhance
the]{.underline} political [credibility of NATO's deterrent by making
the perennial question of whether any U.S. president would]{.underline}
"[trade New York for Paris]{.underline}" **[less
relevant]{.underline}**.

### No Link\-\--Poland\-\--2AC

#### Poland responds by [doubling down]{.underline} on the U.S. alliance.

Jonathan **Paquin &** Pierre **Colautti-Féré 24**, Professor of
Political Science at Laval University, Ph.D. in Political Science from
McGill University; Ph.D. candidate in Political Science at Laval
University, "From attractiveness to hard hedging: US allies' response to
Washington's lack of security assurance under the Obama and Trump
presidencies," Contemporary Politics, Vol. 30, No. 2, 2024, accessed
07-14-2025, https://doi.org/10.1080/13569775.2023.2268880, DG

The [Polish authorities expressed concerns about the reliability of US
engagement]{.underline} in European security throughout the 2010s. This
originated early in Obama's first term when he reset the dialogue with
Russia. In the summer of 2009, President [Obama showed goodwill towards
Russia by cancelling]{.underline} the deployment of the [missile
defence]{.underline} system in Europe, which had long soured relations
between Washington and Moscow (Harding & Traynor, 2009). [This decision
was **poorly received in Warsaw**]{.underline}. During an exchange with
US Senator Carl Levin, Witold Waszczykowski, the Deputy Chief of
President Kaczynski's National Security Bureau, asked, 'how long will it
take \[the United States\] to realise that nothing will change with
\[...\] Russia?' (The Guardian, 2010). Despite the White House's promise
to modernise Polish forces and provide them with Patriot missile
batteries, Polish concerns remain unappeased.

Then, in 2011, Barack Obama announced his Pivot to Asia strategy,
revealing a shift in focus from Europe and its rivalry with Russia to
the Indo-Pacific and its competition with China. With this shift, the
Obama administration expected European partners to shoulder greater
responsibility in addressing security issues on the continent;
consequently raising fear of US abandonment in Polish elites circles.
These concerns were confirmed when the 44th president showed reluctance
to severely sanction against Russia after its 2014 aggression against
Ukraine and the annexation of Crimea. [The lack of US leadership in
responding to threats posed by]{.underline} Vladimir [Putin
indicated]{.underline} that [US retrenchment]{.underline} had already
[impacted US influence in Eastern Europe]{.underline} (Zajac, 2016).
According to Poland's former Minister of Foreign Affairs Adam Rotfeld,
US policy in Europe since 2009 was a massive failure as it facilitated
Russian expansionism, which the Obama administration failed to halt, and
it suggested that NATO was not as strong as it was (Shuster, 2014).

Donald **[Trump]{.underline}** further **[increased Poland's
restlessness]{.underline}**. His [repeated remarks about NATO being
costly and obsolete intensified Poland's concerns about US indifference
towards European security. Warsaw]{.underline} also [grew **fearful of a
potential rapprochement between Trump and Putin** due to the president's
transactional approach to world politics and his perception
of]{.underline} Vladimir [Putin as]{.underline} a [strong]{.underline}
leader. To Poland, this signalled Trump's attempt to prevent the
formation of a Sino-Russian bloc, which would have been done at the
expense of Poland and other European allies' security (Lanoszka, 2020).
[Poland **seriously questioned** Trump's ability to fully grasp Russia's
potential threats]{.underline}.

This [fear of US hegemonic restraint came]{.underline} at a time [when
Russia was increasingly perceived]{.underline} by Poland [as a
significant threat]{.underline}. While the Polish government had
accessed that an opened conflict with Russia was 'only slight' in the
early 2000s (Ministry of National Defence, 2003 p.2), Poland's strategic
landscape underwent a radical transformation in the late 2000s. The 2008
Russo-Georgian War and the largest Russian military exercises con-
ducted at Poland's border in 2009, led Warsaw to conclude having
underestimated the threats posed by Moscow. The annexation of Crimea in
2014 and the war that Moscow man- ufactured in the Donbas region only
served to confirm this analysis. In its 2017 update of its Defence
Concept, the Ministry of Defence acknowledged that 'the scale of threats
resulting from the Russian aggressive policy had not been adequately
assessed in the past,' and '\[t\]his resulted in a wrong conviction that
the risk of an armed conflict in our part of Europe was marginal'
(Ministry of National Defence, 2017, p. 6).

Internal balancing for attractiveness

[Abandonment anxiety and Russia's threat led Poland to make
**substantial investments** in defence]{.underline} throughout the
2010s. Poland's defence budget grew by 86% from 2009 to 2021 to reach 15
billion US dollars (SIPRI, 2023b). The Polish parliament passed a law
mandating the allocation of at least 2% of GDP annually for defence
financing starting in 2016, with a minimum of 20% of this budget
dedicated to modernisation efforts (Zajac, 2016, p. 155). The Polish
government aimed to achieve a defence expenditure of 2.5% of GDP by
2030. Additionally, the [Polish]{.underline} armed forces underwent a
significant transformation with the implementation of the 'Komorowski
Doctrine' in 2015. This [doctrine aimed to modernice and refocus the
Polish armed forces, with a particular emphasis on territorial defence
against Russian threats]{.underline}.

In parallel, [Warsaw **strained** to enlarge the US military footprint
in Poland. It requested greater US military presence]{.underline} on
NATO's eastern flank and expressed interest in a permanent US military
presence within the country. At the 2016 Warsaw Summit, NATO members
agreed first the first time to deploy military forces to eastern Poland.
This included the stationing of a rotational force of 3,000 soldiers and
the deployment of about 1,000 US troops as part of a US-led NATO
enhanced Forward Presence Battlegroup (Emmott & Siebold, 2016). [By
beefing up]{.underline} its [internal defence]{.underline} capabilities
[with American equipment, Warsaw **sought to demonstrate its
credibility** as a reliable ally deserving protection]{.underline}.
Shortly after the Summit, Warsaw dramatically cancelled a \$3 billion
agreement to buy 50 Airbus transport helicopters and instead purchased
US-made Sikorsky helicopters. This not only demonstrated Warsaw
commitment to closer ties with Washington but also garnered increased
support for its request for a greater NATO presence on the alliance's
eastern border (Reuters, 2016b).

Furthermore, and similar to Japan, [Poland's attractiveness strategy was
acute under the Trump presidency]{.underline}. Polish President [Duda
remained **staunchly pro-American and pro-Trump**. Duda even supported
Trump's criticism of the **E**uropean **U**nion, as]{.underline} the
[Polish]{.underline} government's [relations with Brussels faltered
over]{.underline} the issue of [democratic decline]{.underline}
(Montgomery, 2020). In 2018, President Andrzej [Duda]{.underline} even
[chose to play on Trump's narcissism by **proposing**]{.underline} to
finance a permanent base named '**[Fort Trump]{.underline}**' to curry
favour with the US president (Hennigan, 2019). The Polish government
offered to contribute up to US\$2 billion for its construction.
[This]{.underline} proposal, following the dramatic cancellation of the
Airbus helicopters, [exemplified the]{.underline} type of [**internal
balancing for attractiveness** pursued by the Polish government towards
its security patron]{.underline}. Although the project was later
abandoned due to financial and strategic reasons, [Warsaw]{.underline}
nevertheless [convinced Washington to dispatch additional
troops]{.underline} to its territory. In a new bilateral defence
cooperation agreement, the Trump administration formalised its
intentions to provide Poland with additional defence and deterrence
capabilities. The military presence in Poland reached approximately
4,500 rotational US military personnel, with an additional 1,000
military personnel to be added (President of the Republic of Poland,
2019). Shortly after the joint declaration, Poland signed a \$4.6
billion deal with the United States to acquire 32 F-35A Lightning II
fighter jets. The deal included training, logistics, and simulators.
Warsaw also acquired almost a dozen Patriot system batteries. This
agreement was a sign of the strong relationship between Poland and the
United States, and it further strengthened the relationship between the
two countries.

In 2019, Poland went further by launching two Technical Modernisation
Plans with the objective of investing \$133 billion until 2035 in the
acquisition of fifth-generation US combat aircraft, unmanned aerial
vehicles (UAVs), helicopters, short-range rockets, sub- marines, and
cybersecurity. With these plans and previous US military purchased, the
United States became by far Poland's primary military supplier
(Lesiecki, 2019; SIPRI, 2023a; Szopa, 2019). During a 2019 meeting of
NATO defence ministers, Mariusz Błaszczak, the Polish Defence Minister,
summarised the country's strategic stance, stating, 'We assume
solidarity. We are active, which implies that our partners also support
us' (Ministry of National Defence, 2019).

Warsaw's little interest in strategic diversification

Throughout the 2010s, [Warsaw entrusted its security protection to its
most important ally, the United States. **Poland viewed the U**nited
**S**tates **as its only viable partner**, which explains why Warsaw did
not invest]{.underline} more [in strategic diversification.
**Every**]{.underline} foreign and defence [**white paper** emphasised
the **central role of the US**]{.underline} in Poland's security.
Kwiatkowska et al. (2022) show, for instance, that Polish parliamentary
discourses consistently emphasised the importance of the United States,
especially in 2008 and then in 2013- 2014, when Polish political leaders
sought ways to ensure US security in light of Russia's threats.

[Polish leaders did not view the EU as a reliable security
option]{.underline}. As Alexander Lanoszka puts it, '\[o\]f course, [the
EU is not a military organisation]{.underline}, but that is the point.
[**Military power is seen as the bedrock for Polish security**, rather
than values and commerce]{.underline} per se, important as they are'
(2020, p. 463). In fact, throughout the 2010s, Polish leaders feared a
split between their country's interests and those of other EU members.
While Poland focused on military security, Western European countries
concentrated on non- military aspects such as immigration, climate
change, and economic crises (Zajac, 2016, p.138).

Two trends emerged from Poland's strategic thinking. First, [Polish
leaders feared that **any alternative security arrangements**
could]{.underline} potentially [undermine US commitment to Poland's
security. This is why]{.underline} the concept of Europe's [strategic
autonomy]{.underline}, as emphasised by French President Emmanuel
Macron, [raised concerns in Poland. Such a project could upset
Washington, weaken US military engagement in Europe, and prove
detrimental to Poland's interests and security]{.underline}
(ThefirstNews, 2021; Zandee et al., 2020). More generally, [Polish
leaders suspected]{.underline} that [EU security policy and European
members' initiatives would **enable the US to shift** its focus more
towards China and less towards Russia]{.underline}.

Second, [Poland aimed to]{.underline} strengthen European security
institutions and cooperation mainly to **[keep the US
engaged]{.underline}** in regional security. This was [exemplified by
the proposal to cooperate with the US within]{.underline} the EU's
Permanent Structured Cooperation ([PESCO]{.underline}). This strategy of
[institutional binding was]{.underline} essentially [a variation on the
same theme: **ensuring**]{.underline} that [**the U**nited **S**tates
**would continue to be militarily involved** in Eastern
Europe]{.underline}. In this context, Polish leaders also sought to
enhance regional cooperation, particularly with the Visegrad Group
(Czech Republic, Hungary, and Slovakia) and the Baltic states, with the
broader objective of strengthening NATO's Eastern Flank (Ministry of
National Defense, 2017). Poland also perceived EU defence initiatives
such as the European Defence Fund and PESCO as a means to bolster NATO
through European cooperation.

In conclusion, [while Poland heavily invested in internal balancing for
attractiveness, it did not]{.underline} significantly [strengthen its
military ties to the EU. **Warsaw held the belief that instead of
mitigating the effects of U.S. retrenchment, strategic diversification
would**]{.underline} actually **[exacerbate it]{.underline}**. As a
result[, Polish leaders exercised extreme caution by selectively opting
for security initiatives aimed **solely** at strengthening their
security ties with Washington and **keeping their ally more engaged** in
the region]{.underline}.

### No Link\-\--Poland\-\--1AR

#### Poland is [anti-Ukraine]{.underline} and [pro-Russia]{.underline}. 

Piotr **Buras 25**, Head of the Warsaw Office and Senior Policy Fellow
at the European Council on Foreign Relations, "Trump May Unite Europe,
But He Divides Polish Society," Internationale Politik Quarterly,
03-26-2025, accessed 07-18-2025,
https://ip-quarterly.com/en/trump-may-unite-europe-he-divides-polish-society,
DG

[The pro-Russia turn on the part of the US government has]{.underline}
thus [not necessarily persuaded all Poles to feel negatively about
Trump]{.underline}. Rather, [Trump's tough course on Ukraine has
encouraged the right-wing]{.underline} electorate and politicians [to
develop a new approach which is both **pro-American and
anti-Ukrainian**]{.underline}---a novelty in the Polish discourse. For
example, [they blame]{.underline} Ukrainian President Volodymyr
[Zelensky for his]{.underline} alleged "[misbehavior]{.underline}" [in
the Oval Office and defend Trump against criticism]{.underline}; like
the US president, they expect more "gratitude" from Ukraine.

[A well-known **anti-Western-European sentiment** is responsible for
part of this]{.underline} narrative. Former PiS Prime Minister Mateusz
Morawiecki wrote in response to JD Vance's speech to the Munich Security
Conference: "I have no doubt that Western Europe will sacrifice Poland
if necessary." He added: "[When it comes to negotiating the fate of
Central Europe, we could become an appetizer on the menu of Berlin,
Brussels, and Paris]{.underline}."

[Trump's policies]{.underline}, therefore, [are not only challenging
Poland's security policy fundamentals, but also the **long-standing
consensus on Atlanticism**]{.underline} that used to unite the country
beyond party political differences. [Trump may have unified Europeans
around]{.underline} the purpose of a [common defense. But the
centrifugal forces he has unleashed in European societies may prove to
be no less consequential than the **collapse of the US security
guarantee**]{.underline}.

### No Link\-\--Baltics\-\--2AC

#### US not key\-\--Eastern Europe will choose to work with the rest of Europe.

Justina **Budginaite-Froehly 25**, Nonresident Senior Fellow in the
Scowcroft Center for Strategy and Security at the Atlantic Council,
Ph.D. from Martin Luther University Halle-Wittenberg, "How NATO's
eastern flank is setting the standard for collective defense," Atlantic
Council, 05-30-2025, accessed 07-14-2025,
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/how-natos-eastern-flank-is-setting-the-standard-for-collective-defense,
DG

"I am glad to be in Vilnius today," said German Chancellor Friedrich
Merz on May 22. "Because it is right here, [in Lithuania]{.underline},
where [we are taking the defense of NATO's eastern flank **into our own
hands**]{.underline}."

Merz was in Vilnius to formally inaugurate the 45th Armored Brigade in
Lithuania, which will embed German combat power at the heart of Baltic
defense. Germany will implement a phased deployment---it sent advanced
elements in early 2024 and formally activated the brigade on April 1.
The brigade is expected to reach full combat readiness by 2027. Once
complete, this will offer Lithuania and its neighbors a sustained,
high-end deterrent anchored in the NATO framework.

But [it's not just Germany that is helping bolster the defenses of
NATO's]{.underline} strategically exposed [eastern flank. The eastern
flank countries themselves are implementing **concrete measures** to
overcome Europe's entrenched defense fragmentation. Finland, Estonia,
Latvia, Lithuania, and Poland]{.underline}---all located along the
eastern border of both NATO and the European Union (EU) with mainland
Russia, its Kaliningrad exclave, and Belarus---[are emerging as
**leaders in bolstering regional defense integration**]{.underline}, the
benefits of which could extend throughout Europe. [This]{.underline}
shift [is especially significant amid **growing transatlantic tensions**
and **renewed calls from the U**nited **S**tates **for Europe to assume
greater responsibility** for its own security]{.underline} and
conventional defense.

[Since]{.underline} Russia's full-scale invasion of [Ukraine, these
countries have aligned]{.underline} their [border protection
efforts]{.underline} by integrating their counter-mobility measures.
They have [initiated the process of cooperatively developing deep-strike
capabilities]{.underline}. They have [also started the procurement
process for German weapon systems]{.underline}, introduced the German
defense industry to the region, and will soon host the first-ever
permanently deployed German brigade. All [these initiatives
show]{.underline} that [Europe's defense efforts are **well-positioned
to grow together** and **consolidate** from the epicenter in the
northeast of the continent]{.underline}. By anchoring their defense
planning in regional realities, [the eastern flank countries are
demonstrating that regional coordination, backed by political
determination, can **transform**]{.underline} exposed **[vulnerabilities
into strategic assets]{.underline}** [that enhance deterrence and
operational readiness]{.underline}.

### No Link\-\--Baltics\-\--1AR

#### Enhanced counter-mobility proves.

Justina **Budginaite-Froehly 25**, Nonresident Senior Fellow in the
Scowcroft Center for Strategy and Security at the Atlantic Council,
Ph.D. from Martin Luther University Halle-Wittenberg, "How NATO's
eastern flank is setting the standard for collective defense," Atlantic
Council, 05-30-2025, accessed 07-14-2025,
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/how-natos-eastern-flank-is-setting-the-standard-for-collective-defense,
DG

[Faced with growing]{.underline} geostrategic [pressure along their
borders with Russia and Belarus, Finland, Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania,
and Poland have taken **decisive, coordinated steps** to reinforce
border protection and defense]{.underline}. Increasingly aligned in
their strategic approach, these countries are developing integrated
fortification systems that form a continuous defensive line along the
eastern border of NATO and the EU.

[Two major initiatives]{.underline} launched in 2024---[the Baltic
Defense Line]{.underline} covering Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania, [and
Poland's East Shield]{.underline}---[share the common goal of
strengthening deterrence and denying adversaries access to NATO and EU
territory]{.underline}. These efforts include expanding existing
forested areas, deepening drainage ditches, building engineering depots
to store physical barriers such as "dragons' teeth," "hedgehogs,"
"Spanish horse," and solid concrete road barriers, as well as installing
anti-tank landmines and mine-laying equipment. [There are]{.underline}
also [plans for developing reinforcements with drone and anti-drone
technologies]{.underline}. The overall goal is [to ensure the two
defense initiatives' integrity]{.underline}, especially [as they
converge at the Suwałki Gap, a security chokepoint and the primary axis
for NATO's land reinforcement to the Baltic states]{.underline}.

Finland's accession to NATO added 1,340 kilometers to the Alliance's
border with Russia. Unlike the more exposed terrain of the Baltic states
and Poland, Finland's border region is naturally defensive, dominated by
forests, lakes, and wetlands, which would complicate a large-scale
Russian ground incursion. Accordingly, Finland has chosen not to build
physical fortifications along the border.

However, [to reinforce deterrence and secure vulnerable segments,
Finland aligned itself with]{.underline} the [regional consensus by
withdrawing from the Ottawa Convention]{.underline} banning the use of
anti-personnel landmines. This means the [eastern flank countries can
jointly deploy and stockpile anti-personnel landmines as a shared border
defense tool against Russia]{.underline}. Both Russia and Ukraine have
used anti-personnel landmines in Ukraine.

The [regional integration of border defense has triggered **broader EU
interest**. Following Baltic and Polish calls for a collective response,
the European Council's conclusions on European defense]{.underline},
released on March 6, [recognized the importance of EU border
defense]{.underline}. The EU's White Paper on Defense, published in late
March, endorsed the idea of creating an "Eastern Border Shield" and in
April, the European Parliament passed a resolution recognizing the East
Shield and the Baltic Defense Line as flagship projects for common
security. This momentum must now translate into concrete EU
support---and funding---for transforming the eastern flank countries'
national efforts into a unified, layered European border defense
architecture.

#### Deep strike. 

Justina **Budginaite-Froehly 25**, Nonresident Senior Fellow in the
Scowcroft Center for Strategy and Security at the Atlantic Council,
Ph.D. from Martin Luther University Halle-Wittenberg, "How NATO's
eastern flank is setting the standard for collective defense," Atlantic
Council, 05-30-2025, accessed 07-14-2025,
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/how-natos-eastern-flank-is-setting-the-standard-for-collective-defense,
DG

[The Baltic states, Poland, and Finland are]{.underline} also
[integrating]{.underline} their [long-range firepower into a **regional
deep-strike architecture**, which **significantly raises the threshold
for aggression** along the eastern flank of NATO and the
EU]{.underline}.

[Since]{.underline} Russia launched its full-scale war against Ukraine
in [2022, Estonia, Latvia, and Lithuania]{.underline} have each
[contracted]{.underline} High Mobility Artillery Rocket Systems
([HIMARS]{.underline}) [launchers and committed to trilateral
**coop**eration with the **U**nited **S**tates]{.underline} on HIMARS
integration, personnel training, system maintenance, and service.
Embedding [HIMARS]{.underline} into a joint operational concept [will
allow the Baltic states to conduct combined live-fire exercises and
harmonize sustainment]{.underline} through shared maintenance, training,
and logistics chains. Estonia's six launchers, delivered in April, now
reach well beyond four hundred kilometers. Lithuania's eight launchers,
due to arrive by 2026, will achieve a similar reach. Latvia is set to
receive six launchers in 2027, which will complete the Baltic
deep-strike firewall.

[This Baltic cluster is dovetailing with Poland']{.underline}s even
larger "[Homar-A]{.underline}" [deep-strike program]{.underline}, under
which some 486 additional HIMARS variants will be mounted on Polish
Jelcz trucks and integrated into Poland's Topaz command system.
Together, [Poland and the Baltic states are planning a joint logistics
hub to manage munitions stockpiles, spare parts, and forward
displacement]{.underline}. They are also planning to exercise joint
targeting and coordinate fire support across borders.

[Finland]{.underline} has [opted to upgrade its]{.underline} M270
[multiple-launch rocket systems]{.underline} rather than buy HIMARS. The
upgrade, approved in 2023, allows Finnish M270s to fire the same
munitions as their Baltic neighbors. This technical alignment transforms
Finland's forces into a seamless fourth pillar of the regional
deep-strike ensemble, enabling integrated planning, data‐sharing, and
cross-border reinforcement exercises.

By integrating US-provided launchers, coordinated doctrine, shared
logistics, and interoperable fire-control standards, [the five eastern
flank nations are establishing a continuous, multi-tiered long-range
fire network that **spans from Finland to Poland**. This]{.underline}
network [helps project deterrence and complicate adversary planning,
**solidifying**]{.underline} a new level of [**collective defense
integration** on the eastern flank]{.underline}.

#### German defense partnerships. 

Justina **Budginaite-Froehly 25**, Nonresident Senior Fellow in the
Scowcroft Center for Strategy and Security at the Atlantic Council,
Ph.D. from Martin Luther University Halle-Wittenberg, "How NATO's
eastern flank is setting the standard for collective defense," Atlantic
Council, 05-30-2025, accessed 07-14-2025,
https://www.atlanticcouncil.org/blogs/new-atlanticist/how-natos-eastern-flank-is-setting-the-standard-for-collective-defense,
DG

[The eastern flank countries]{.underline} have also [deepened their
partnerships with German weapon manufacturers. Lithuania is aligning its
force modernization with the German brigade's forward posture in the
Baltics]{.underline}. In December, Lithuania's defense ministry signed a
€950 million contract with for forty-four Leopard 2 A8 main battle
tanks---its first indigenous tank battalion---which will arrive through
2030, alongside an expanded fleet of twenty-seven additional Boxer
"Vilkas" infantry fighting vehicles, which will arrive by 2029. [Finland
and Poland]{.underline} likewise [use Leopard 2 variants, creating a
shared main battle tank backbone]{.underline} across the eastern flank.

In the air defense domain, [Estonia and Latvia will each field three
IRIS-T surface-launched missile batteries this year]{.underline}, while
[Lithuania has bolstered its]{.underline} two national advanced
surface-to-air missile systems ([NASAMS]{.underline})
[batteries]{.underline} (initially deployed in 2020) [with additional
systems due to arrive in 2026]{.underline}. To underpin sustained
operations, German arms manufacturer Rheinmetall's new NATO-standard 155
mm ammunition plant in Lithuania, scheduled to be online by mid-2026,
will produce tens of thousands of shells annually, significantly
enhancing regional munitions resilience.

In the defense innovation field, [Lithuanian laser technology firm
Aktyvus Photonics has partnered with German drone manufacturer Quantum
Systems]{.underline}, having signed a memorandum of understanding on
strategic collaboration in unmanned systems this month. Together, they
will codevelop and field-test unmanned aerial vehicles equipped with
laser capabilities, aiming to expedite deployment timelines and
establish a standard for next-generation, networked unmanned systems in
NATO's eastern defenses.

[Beyond procurement, production, and innovation, Lithuania]{.underline}
has also [positioned itself as a **regional arms maintenance hub**:
Through the establishment of Lithuania Defense Services]{.underline}---a
joint venture between Rheinmetall Landsysteme and French-German defense
manufacturer KNDS---[it provides repair, overhaul, and upgrade services
for German vehicle platforms]{.underline}, including Boxer Vilkas IFVs,
PzH 2000 howitzers, [the Leopard main battle tank family, and tactical
logistics vehicles]{.underline}.

Collectively, [these deployments, procurements, and industrial
partnerships do more than fill capability gaps: They forge a
**contiguous eastern flank defense ecosystem**. By standardizing on
German platforms, harmonizing training and logistics, co-locating
production and repair facilities, and co-training under unified command
structures, Finland, the Baltic states, and Poland, together with
Germany, can achieve **unprecedented interoperability and strategic
depth**. These initiatives are transforming NATO's eastern flank into a
**seamless, multi-domain bulwark**]{.underline}.

Strategic depth through regional alignment

[The eastern flank's transformation from]{.underline} a collection of
[fragmented national postures into a cohesive, multi-domain defense
network exemplifies how sustained regional integration can **overcome
long-standing capability gaps**]{.underline}. By aligning border
defense, harmonizing long-range fires, and embedding German heavy armor
and sustainment infrastructure, [Finland, Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania,
and Poland are creating a **continuous belt of deterrence** that
leverages shared doctrine, logistics, and industry. This]{.underline}
holistic approach [raises the cost of aggression and sets a new standard
for European collective defense]{.underline}: one [where
interoperability and joint capacity-building replace duplication and
dependency, anchoring strategic depth at NATO's most exposed
frontier]{.underline}.

### No Link\-\--AT: Cred Theory\-\--2AC

#### Credibility theory is [wrong]{.underline}\-\--perception of U.S. commitment differs among allies.

Joshua **Alley &** Brian **Blankenship 25**, Assistant Professor of
Politics and International Relations at University College Dublin, Ph.D.
in Political Science from Texas A&M University; Assistant Professor of
Political Science at the University of Miami, Ph.D. in Political Science
from Columbia University, "Is Alliance Credibility Interdependent?", OSF
Preprints, 01-15-2025, accessed 07-18-2025,
https://doi.org/10.31219/osf.io/p59ju, DG

Our findings support conditional credibility arguments. [Alliance
credibility is]{.underline} strongly correlated among close allies, but
**[weakly correlated across regions]{.underline}**. This implies that
alliance credibility is interdependent, but not across all alliances.

Therefore, [there is little evidence for the strongest claims about
interdependent or independent alliance credibility. We find **little
evidence** of]{.underline} that [meaningful correlations in credibility
span regions]{.underline}. As a result, [upholding credibility in one
region **will have at best a marginal relationship with credibility in
more distant alliances**]{.underline}. But [allies that are close to one
another do exhibit meaningful correlations]{.underline}, so reduced
credibility in one treaty could undermine others. The results likewise
provide further evidence on the importance of regions as a distinctive
level of analysis in international relations (Buzan and Wæver 2003).

The results have policy implications as well. [Policymakers]{.underline}
who are [considering reducing alliance commitments can expect that lost
credibility in one treaty will have the **greatest impact on proximate
partners**]{.underline}. But **[downgrading U.S. commitments in one
region might not cause perceived credibility in other regions to
decline]{.underline}**. The [results]{.underline} thus
[suggest]{.underline} there is [reason to be optimistic that
prioritizing alliance commitments in one region over another will not
come at the expense of a tarnished reputation elsewhere. To the extent
that opposition to retrenchment and support for]{.underline}
strengthening or maintaining [the status quo in U.S. alliances draw on
concern]{.underline} that [allies make inferences about American
credibility across regions, our findings suggest **this concern may be
misplaced**]{.underline}.

#### Cred theory is [historically]{.underline} and [academically]{.underline} bankrupt.

Stephen M. **Walt 22**, Robert and Renee Belfer Professor of
International Affairs at Harvard University, Ph.D. in Political Science
from the University of California, Berkeley, "America Has an Unhealthy
Obsession With Credibility," Foreign Policy, 01-29-2022,
https://foreignpolicy.com/2022/01/29/us-credibility-ukraine-russia-grand-strategy

As many observers have commented, [the most predictable feature of any
debate about U.S. foreign policy is the ritual **invoking**
of]{.underline} "**[credibility]{.underline}**." Whether the issue is
Ukraine, Iran, Russia, Taiwan, Afghanistan, the global war on terror, or
even trade policy, sooner or later someone is going to argue that
failing to implement their preferred policy will do grievous harm to the
United States' reputation for resolve.

Why is the United States so obsessed with this aspect of foreign policy?
Is it an artifact of its political culture, a result of how
international relations is taught in universities, or an odd quirk of
America's national history? Is it merely a handy refuge for hard-liners
trying to justify actions that can't be defended on other grounds or
just a persistent mind-worm that the foreign-policy elite can't get
past? Or is it perhaps the direct consequence of U.S. grand strategy
itself?

Whatever the reason, [I can't think of another great power as
concerned]{.underline} with preserving its own credibility [as the
**U**nited **S**tates]{.underline} has been for many years. Don't get me
wrong: I'm not saying the United States is the only country that worries
about its reputation for resolve, nor am I suggesting that being
perceived as credible isn't valuable when dealing with friends and foes
alike. Since World War II, however, [the idea that a failure to respond
to and decisively defeat any challenge would have baleful
consequences]{.underline} for the United States [has been a central
theme]{.underline} of U.S. foreign-policy discourse. It is the key
ingredient in the infamous Munich analogy---surely the most moth-eaten
trope in the foreign-policy elite's toolkit---but also central to the
so-called domino theory and recurring fear that any sign of weakness
would lead allies to abandon the United States and bandwagon to its
foes.

In 1968, for example, then-incoming U.S. National Security Advisor Henry
Kissinger insisted that the United States had to fight on in Vietnam,
even though he had apparently believed the United States could not hope
to win the war. Why? Because he believed U.S. credibility was
all-important: In his words, "nations can gear their actions to ours
only if they can count on our steadiness."

Failing to respond when states act contrary to U.S. wishes supposedly
invites further predations, which is why [some observers of]{.underline}
former U.S. President Barack [Obama's]{.underline} [decision not to
retaliate after]{.underline} Syrian President Bashar [al-Assad used
chemical weapons encouraged]{.underline} Russian President Vladimir
[Putin]{.underline} to seize Crimea in 2014, [even though direct
evidence of a link is **lacking**]{.underline} and [Putin might well
have acted **no matter what Obama did**]{.underline}. Today, former U.S.
officials claim that failing to respond with force to a Russian attack
on Ukraine will invite a Chinese attack on Taiwan and "set the global
system back decades," said retired U.S. Navy Adm. James Stavridis to the
New York Times.

[For the credibility-obsessed]{.underline}, in short, **[everything is
connected to everything]{.underline}** else, and [even fairly minor
events]{.underline} in one part of the world can [have enormous
repercussions **thousands of miles away**]{.underline}. Take this logic
far enough and the credibility of U.S. commitments becomes the single
most important source of peace and order in the world. Historian Hal
Brands, U.S. diplomat Eric Edelman, and author Thomas Mahnken express
this hard-line view perfectly:

"If America's credibility is strong, then adversaries will be deterred,
allies will be reassured, and relative geopolitical stability will
prevail. If American credibility is weak, then adversaries will be
emboldened, allies will be unnerved, and geopolitical revisionism and
aggression will proliferate. Opportunistic powers will gradually become
more assertive on the theory that their aggression will not be punished;
the international system will veer toward greater conflict and upheaval.
... \[I\]t is the very foundation of international peace and stability."

[This]{.underline} rather **[self-centered view]{.underline}** of world
affairs conveniently [ignores]{.underline} those [episodes where it was
the **U**nited **S**tates that acted to disrupt]{.underline}
"international [peace]{.underline} and stability," but never mind that
for now. [If the credibility of U.S. promises is the key]{.underline} to
world peace, [one wonders why the world didn't come unglued after the
United States **lost in Vietnam**, after **No**rth **Ko**rea got away
with **seizing the USS Pueblo**]{.underline} and holding its crew
prisoner for almost a year, [after the Shah of Iran fell and the U.S.
**Embassy in Tehran was captured**, after the Reagan administration's
hasty **withdrawal from Lebanon**]{.underline} in 1984,
[after]{.underline} the **[Black Hawk Down]{.underline}** debacle in
Somalia, [or after]{.underline} former U.S. President Bill [Clinton
opted not to intervene in the **Rwanda**n genocide]{.underline}. Maybe
U.S. credibility is neither as fragile nor as essential as hard-liners
think.

More importantly, [this view assumes]{.underline} that [security
guarantees]{.underline}---in particular, guarantees issued by the United
States---[are the main ingredient in global peace, leaving]{.underline}
little or [no role for negotiation, compromise, reassurance, or **other
diplomatic efforts**]{.underline} to give potential rivals less reason
to challenge the status quo. It is also worth noting that the [positive
effects of]{.underline} a successful defense of [credibility
**never**]{.underline} seem to **[last]{.underline}** very long: [No
matter what the **U**nited **S**tates did the last time]{.underline} it
was challenged, it [has to respond as soon as a new problem
emerges]{.underline} or else its newly won reputation for resolve may
shatter like glass.

As you might expect, scholars have explored these issues for years
without reaching a clear consensus. Credibility skeptics---such as
academics Jonathan Mercer, Ted Hopf, and Daryl Press---argue that
concerns about [**cred**ibility are generally **exaggerated**,
especially when]{.underline} would-be [challengers are trying to
determine if another state will fight]{.underline} to defend a
commitment. They point out that [government officials rarely
base]{.underline} their [estimates]{.underline} of what another state
will do [on]{.underline} the basis of [how that state acted in some
**unrelated context** in the past]{.underline}. Rather, [they base them
on]{.underline} whether it has [interests]{.underline} at stake that are
[worth defending and]{.underline} the [capacity to act]{.underline}
effectively. Other scholars have challenged this conclusion, however,
arguing that [**individual leaders** acquire reputations for
resolve]{.underline} (or weakness) that persist over time and that past
behavior can influence how potential challengers gauge a defender's
level of interest in a given commitment. States with good reputations
may find it easier to form alliances too. There is also the strong
possibility that reputation and credibility are issue-specific: Whether
a state lives up to the terms of a trade deal may tell you next to
nothing about its reliability as an ally or on an issue like arms
control.

The bottom line is that credibility and reputation operate in complex
and contingent ways, and [simple generalizations]{.underline} about
them---such as the sort that politicians and pundits typically
make---[are]{.underline} often **[wrong]{.underline}**. Credibility and
reputation do matter in some circumstances, but how, why, and under what
conditions remain exceedingly difficult to parse. (For a good summary of
recent scholarly developments on this topic, see here.)

I won't try to sort out this complicated set of issues here. Instead, I
want to suggest that the U.S. preoccupation with credibility is, to a
large extent, a function of U.S. geopolitical position and grand
strategy, especially the outsized international responsibilities the
United States has assumed for itself.

At the most basic level, credibility is the likelihood that a state will
fulfill a promise or commitment as perceived by others. With respect to
deterrence, for example, a threat to retaliate is credible if a
potential challenger believes it is likely to be carried out. Even when
a defending state is 100 percent willing to fulfill a particular
commitment, deterrence can still fail if the challenger isn't convinced.

Commitments are not created equal, however. [Some guarantees are
**inherently more credible**]{.underline} than others [because it is
obviously in a state's interest to uphold them]{.underline}. No one
questions whether great powers will defend their own soil against
attack, for example, and even weak states often fight fiercely to defend
their home territory against more powerful invaders. Similarly, states
are more likely to fight to defend important economic interests, such as
access to critical resources. For this reason, U.S. pledges to defend
access to energy supplies from the Persian Gulf were inherently credible
because a significant reduction in energy exports from that region might
be extremely costly. One might say something similar about the
importance of defending Taiwan's chip-making industry, which much of the
world now depends on. During the Cold War, the United States' commitment
to defend its allies in Europe and Asia was also highly credible,
especially if it could be done without using nuclear weapons, because
significant Soviet expansion in either region might have tilted the
global balance of power against the United States.

By contrast, [challengers are more likely to question a commitment when
it is more difficult]{.underline} for them [to see it as vital to the
defender. The U.S. pledge to defend West Berlin]{.underline} during the
Cold War [was harder to demonstrate]{.underline}, for example, [because
the loss of West Berlin would not have affected the balance of
power]{.underline} at all or had much of an impact on U.S. prosperity
back home. West Berlin was an important symbol, of course, and U.S.
leaders worked hard to make the pledge to defend it credible by
explicitly tying it to the broader issue of Western resolve. They also
kept enough troops there to make it impossible for the Warsaw Pact to
seize the city without killing a lot of U.S. soldiers. A credible
commitment was maintained, but it took more effort.

In short, [the more tenuous the link between a]{.underline} particular
[pledge and a country's security]{.underline} and prosperity, [the
**harder it will be to convince others**]{.underline} that [the
commitment is rock-solid]{.underline}. In these situations, defenders
are in effect trying to convince others that they are willing to do
something that might not be in their short-term interest---such as being
willing to do something where the short-term costs exceed the
benefits---to convince challengers that their word is good and other
commitments should not be threatened. They aren't necessarily bluffing,
but others are more likely to suspect this might be the case.

Moreover, the greater the number of less-than-vital interests a country
pledges to defend---that is, the more places it says it may fight to
protect for less-than-vital stakes---the more other states will question
both its willingness and ability to do all that it has promised. Trying
to honor all those commitments may also be expensive, sapping both
resolve and resources over time. Instead of bolstering one's
credibility, defending a lot of secondary interests for the sake of
one's future reputation may unintentionally undermine it.

Here's the real kicker: The United States has a credibility problem in
part because its own geopolitical position is so favorable. There are
relatively few interests that are truly vital to the United States'
independence or prosperity, yet it still maintains a far-flung global
presence and has made a lot of promises to protect other countries.

Start with the first point. [By any measure, the **U**nited **S**tate is
still the **most secure great power in history**]{.underline}. It
possesses [the world's largest economy]{.underline} in nominal terms,
there are [no]{.underline} great-power [rivals near its borders, its
nuclear deterrent consists of thousands of sophisticated **nuc**lear
weapon**s**, and it has powerful conventional]{.underline} military
[forces]{.underline}. No country is going to attack the U.S. homeland
directly, attempt to blockade it, or directly threaten the American way
of life. These features do not insulate Americans from all global
dangers---including contagious viruses---but what country wouldn't trade
places with the United States if it could?

On the one hand, this favorable geopolitical position makes it possible
for the United States to intervene all over the world for the simple
reason that it doesn't have to worry very much about defending its own
territory. But on the other hand, [this also means it has **less
immediate need** to take on ambitious foreign-policy missions.
U.S.]{.underline} military deployments and overseas [commitments
aren't]{.underline} primarily [about defending U.S. soil]{.underline}
directly or protecting American lives at home. Rather, the[y are about
trying to shape events in distant regions in ways]{.underline} that
[U.S. leaders think]{.underline} will [spread]{.underline} a desirable
set of [values or enhance U.S. security]{.underline}---or both.

What this situation implies, however, is that there are few overseas
commitments where it is unmistakably clear to friends, foes, and itself
why the United States should fight for them. Please note: I'm not
arguing for isolationism here; as I've written repeatedly, I think the
United States has a vital interest in helping preserve favorable
balances of power in key strategic areas (specifically, East Asia,
Europe, and---to a lesser extent---the Persian Gulf). I am simply
pointing out that potential challengers have reasons to wonder if every
one of the United States' overseas pledges is something the country
would truly be willing to send its children to die for.

During the Cold War, the United States took on an array of global
missions, most of them directly linked to its broad strategy of
containment. But U.S. ambitions grew even larger once the Cold War was
over, and [Washington spent]{.underline} the next [three decades trying
to create a global liberal order under its own]{.underline} supposedly
benevolent [leadership]{.underline}. Today, [there are dozens of places
where the **U**nited **S**tates is engaged]{.underline}, committed,
obligated, [and]{.underline} in some way [expected to act]{.underline}
should trouble emerge [but where it is **hard to demonstrate that truly
vital interests are at stake**]{.underline}. In these circumstances,
[other states are **bound to question** whether the **U**nited
**S**tates would really do]{.underline} what it has promised to do or
whether it would stay the course should fulfilling the stated mission
prove more difficult than expected. That is why U.S. leaders worry so
much about credibility and why they look for opportunities to
demonstrate resolve. They are hoping that a demonstration of firmness
will convince other challengers not to try their luck, thereby making it
less likely that other commitments will need to be defended.

The danger, of course, is that [this]{.underline} powerful [need to
demonstrate credibility will lead U.S. leaders to]{.underline} fight in
[places that **don't matter** very much]{.underline} in the hopes it
makes it less likely that they will have to fight in places that do. But
there's another option: The United States can make its commitments and
guarantees more credible to others by being more careful and
discriminating when making them. A more selective, realistic, rational,
and restrained approach to overseas commitments will make the ones that
remain more credible because the United States' interest in meeting them
will be readily apparent to potential challengers. [Carefully drawn
commitments are]{.underline} also [more likely to command]{.underline}
more enthusiastic [support]{.underline} from the American people, not
just when answering a pollster's question but also when they are called
on to make genuine sacrifices to fulfill them.

### Reassurance Solves\-\--2AC

#### Reassurances prevent prolif.

Nicholas **Miller 24**, Associate Professor of Government at Dartmouth
College, Ph.D. from the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, "The
Future of Nuclear Proliferation after the War in Ukraine," French
Institute of International Relations, Proliferation Papers, No. 67,
April 2024, accessed 07-20-2025,
https://www.ifri.org/sites/default/files/migrated_files/documents/atoms/files/ifri_miller_future_nuclear_proliferation_2024_0.pdf,
DG

[Concerns about US reliability]{.underline} as an ally [go back to
the]{.underline} beginning of the [Cold War]{.underline}. They were
[particularly]{.underline} acute in the late 1950s and early
1960s---[when the US homeland became increasingly
vulnerable]{.underline} to Soviet nuclear attack---and the late 1960s
through early 1970s, when US efforts at détente and retrenchment called
its reliability into question, particularly in Asia.60 In both cases,
[some US allies did consider acquiring their own nuclear
weapons]{.underline} in response. However, [the US **effectively managed
these concerns and prevented prolif**eration]{.underline}, with the
partial exception of France (as discussed below).

The Soviet launch of the Sputnik satellite in October 1957 and
subsequent Soviet development of an ICBM capability roused significant
concerns among NATO allies, who wondered whether Washington would be
willing to fight a war to protect them if it exposed the US homeland to
Soviet nuclear attack. Shortly after the launch, for instance, France,
West Germany, and Italy began discussing the joint production of nuclear
weapons, which would give Europe an independent deterrent outside US
control.61 While this arrangement broke down under De Gaulle, West
Germany and France maintained nuclear ambitions, with Paris racing
forward on an independent nuclear weapons program and Bonn keeping its
nuclear options open.62 Ultimately, [the US succeeded at convincing West
Germany to renounce nuclear weapons through]{.underline} a combination
of **[coercion]{.underline}**---[making clear an alliance with the US
was incompatible with an independent]{.underline} German nuclear
[arsenal]{.underline}---[and **reassurance**, including the creation of
the **N**uclear **P**lanning **G**roup]{.underline}, which involved West
Germany in NATO nuclear strategy discussions.63 [Italy]{.underline}
likewise [accepted]{.underline} the [forward deployment]{.underline} of
US nuclear weapons, which would be [launched under a dual-key
arrangement, as an alternative to an independent]{.underline} or joint
European [deterrent]{.underline}.64

France, of course, ultimately did acquire its own nuclear weapons,
rejecting the Eisenhower administration's offer of forward-deployed US
nuclear weapons as an alternative.65 While this is often attributed to
the sort of alliance concerns described above, with French leaders not
willing to trust the US to protect them, France's nuclear weapons
program pre-dated Sputnik (and the prior Suez Crisis) by several years.
Further, there is significant evidence suggesting French decision-makers
were largely driven by status concerns---in particular, cementing
France's position as a great power---in seeking their own nuclear
arsenal.66

A second round of concerns about US reliability emerged a decade later.
In the context of pressures for retrenchment due to the Vietnam War, the
Nixon administration pushed its Asian allies to do more to provide for
their own defense---the so-called "Nixon doctrine"---and sought improved
relations with Communist China.67 These [policy changes led South Korea
to initiate a secre]{.underline}t nuclear weapons [program, led Taiwan
to accelerate a nascent program, and caused Japan to revive
consideration]{.underline} of an independent nuclear arsenal.68 [Through
a mix of carrots and sticks, **the US succeeded** in convincing South
Korea and Taiwan to end their nuclear weapons programs]{.underline},69
while [**reassurance** was utilized to]{.underline} help [reinforce
Japan's decision to keep its nuclear capabilities latent]{.underline}.70

[The US track record of preventing allied proliferation despite alliance
credibility concerns is]{.underline} thus **[quite strong, even in
periods where the US was perceived to be in relative
decline]{.underline}**, as it was in the early 1960s and early 1970s.
While France's acquisition of nuclear weapons is an important
counterexample, as noted above, concerns about US reliability were
probably not the decisive factor in leading it to acquire nuclear
weapons. The type of [status concerns that motivated French leaders are
unlikely to lead to proliferation amongst US allies today]{.underline};
while nuclear weapons were markers of prestige in the 1950s and early
1960s, [the emergence of the NPT has]{.underline}, for the most part,
**[flipped the script]{.underline}**, meaning [new proliferators can
expect to lose status and be branded as]{.underline}
"[rogue]{.underline}" states for seeking nuclear weapons.71
Nevertheless, for certain types of leaders, for example, highly
nationalist ones like Turkey's Erdogan, nuclear weapons may retain
appeal.72

### NATO Resilient\-\--2AC

#### NATO [survives]{.underline}, even without the United States. 

Andrew **Adonis 25**, Labour Member of the House of Lords in the British
Parliament, Ph.D. from Nuffield College, University of Oxford, "The
essence of Nato will survive," Prospect Magazine, 02-19-2025, accessed
07-18-2025,
https://www.prospectmagazine.co.uk/politics/the-insider/69312/the-essence-of-nato-will-survive,
DG

Don't be too alarmed. **[The essence of Nato will
survive]{.underline}**, maybe not in its current form but certainly as a
security and military alliance embracing Europe's democracies and other
allies. However, incredibly, [the **U**nited **S**tates may soon not be
among these allies if Trump follows through on the threats and
imprecations against democratic Europe made by]{.underline} vice
president JD [Vance and]{.underline} defense secretary Pete
[Hegseth]{.underline} at last week's Munich Secretary Conference.

**[Either Nato or a direct successor organisation will
survive]{.underline}**, even if its only North American member is
Canada, [because the threat]{.underline} of invasion and destabilisation
[from Russia has not dissipated]{.underline}. And [such an organisation
is]{.underline} essential for European democracies to be able to pledge
collective security and pool their military efforts, separate from---but
[intimately linked to]{.underline}---[the **E**uropean
**U**nion]{.underline}, whose remit is economic not military.

When Nato was founded in 1949 by Ernest Bevin and George Marshall, the
foreign ministers of Britain and the US, the imperative was to prevent
Josef Stalin taking over western Europe, as he had most of central and
eastern Europe. Britain and the US had just held on to the British and
American zones of West Berlin---within the territory of
Soviet-controlled eastern Germany---after Stalin's bitterly fought
Berlin Blockade. They were determined to prevent further incursions, and
in particular to protect the nascent West Germany, which for obvious
reasons lacked an army.

[Nato has **succeeded spectacularly** as a defensive
alliance]{.underline}. By 1955 Stalin was dead, West Germany and Turkey
had joined Nato, the Russians had withdrawn from Austria and western
Europe was secure. [Nato was an indispensable part of the west's
resistance to Soviet communism]{.underline} until the fall of the Berlin
Wall in 1989, after which almost all of the formerly Soviet-controlled
central and eastern European states---including the newly united
Germany---joined the Nato club.

For a brief period in the 1990s, it looked as if Nato might become
largely redundant because Boris Yeltsin's Russia itself might become a
western democracy. Russia might then have joined Nato too, alongside
former Soviet republics including Ukraine. But Yeltsin's successor was
Vladimir Putin, and the rest is history.

[The key point is that, Ukraine apart, the entire European border of
Russia]{.underline} now [comprises Nato members]{.underline}---including
Finland which, together with Sweden, rushed to join after Putin's
invasion of Ukraine three years ago. It is **[only Nato's collective
strength]{.underline}** which **[gives credibility to the defence of
this long border against an increasingly belligerent Russia. So with or
without the US, if Nato did not exist, it would have to be
created]{.underline}**. And [this collective entity will]{.underline}
need to [enhance Europe's engagement with free Ukraine to prevent a
Russian takeover]{.underline}.

Trump is now weighing up whether his preferred partner is democratic
Europe or Putin's Russia---or an opportunist combination of the two.
This incredible geopolitical crisis is being played out in the
negotiations over the fate of Ukraine. [If Trump abandons Ukraine,
**Europe will**]{.underline} have to **[step up and]{.underline}** in
effect **[take over Nato, with or without the US]{.underline}** as a
continuing member. [The alternative]{.underline}---leaving Europe
without any collective defence---[is unconscionable]{.underline}. This
is the significance of the European leaders coming together for an
emergency meeting in Paris this Monday, in reaction to [Vance and
Hegseth's alarming speeches]{.underline} and statements in Munich. Taken
together, their speeches [amount to a **massive appeasement of Putin**
and a huge question mark over Trump's commitment]{.underline} not only
to Ukraine but [to the wider defence of Europe]{.underline}.

The immediate emergency issue is how to protect the currently unoccupied
majority of Ukraine from falling to Putin. It is imperative to prevent
an outright Russian victory in the current war if it continues and the
US withdraws support. It is equally important to be able to defend
Ukraine effectively against renewed Russian aggression should there be a
ceasefire. Either way, Europe is going to have to make a much larger
contribution to collective defence.

But there is another possibility. Great dictators often fall out. Maybe
the current Trump/Putin bromance turns sour and/or the threat from China
becomes too serious for the US to risk a complete rupture with Europe on
trade and defence. [There are still plenty of Washington
bigwigs]{.underline}, including Republican politicians and military and
economic strategists, [whose heart is firmly with the Atlantic alliance.
**They may be down, but they are not yet out**]{.underline}.

## Impact\-\--General

### No Prolif\-\--2AC

#### No [European proliferation]{.underline}.

**Naughtie '25** \[Andrew; March 21; MA in Social Sciences, University
of Chicago; Euro News, "Could another European country develop its own

https://www.euronews.com/2025/03/21/could-another-european-country-develop-its-own-nuclear-weapons;

[Although some European states have **some** of the elements required to
develop independent nuclear weapons capability]{.underline},
[**experts** say the chances of another European state going nuclear are
**slim**]{.underline}.

Starting from scratch

According to Fabian Rene Hoffmann, a research fellow at the Oslo Nuclear
Project, [even if one of Europe's NATO powers were keen to develop its
**own nuclear weapons** rather than simply **hosting
them**]{.underline}, [it would find itself at a **standing
start**]{.underline}.

"[The major issue European countries are facing is that they either
don't deploy the civilian nuclear infrastructure to launch a nuclear
**weapons programme**]{.underline}, or, if they have civilian nuclear
infrastructure, that [it is highly]{.underline}
'**[proliferation-resistant']{.underline}**," he told Euronews.

"For example, Finland and Sweden only have light-water reactors, which
are not suitable for the production of weapons-grade plutonium. In
addition, n[either of those countries have chemical reprocessing plants
that are needed for separating wanted from **unwanted isotopes** in
**fissile material production**]{.underline}.\"

\"So even if they wanted to launch a nuclear program, [they couldn't do
so with their **existing infrastructure**]{.underline}, at least in the
short-term. [That's the case for **all** non-nuclear weapon states in
**Europe**]{.underline} with a civilian nuclear programme right now."

Hoffman acknowledged one arguable exception: Germany.

"While it does not deploy significant civilian nuclear infrastructure
anymore, it has a large stockpile of highly-enriched uranium for
research purposes,\" he explained. \"Theoretically, this stockpile could
be repurposed under some effort to create weapons-grade fissile
material."

"[But **even then** it would only be enough for around 5 to 15 nuclear
warheads]{.underline}, so [it would not be enough to deploy what we call
a]{.underline} "**[robust]{.underline}**" **[nuclear
deterrent]{.underline}**.\"

Opening the umbrella

Both of Europe's nuclear-armed powers, the UK and France, have big
decisions to make about their nuclear futures.

As things stand, the UK's limited submarine-based deterrent is facing an
uncertain future. The fleet used to carry its missiles is ageing and set
to be replaced, and more pressingly still, the missiles themselves are
made and stockpiled at a US base, meaning the British deterrent is
unusually reliant on the participation of another state.

France's deterrent, by contrast, is larger and more independent of NATO,
and is not confined to submarines. That means it could potentially be
"forward-deployed" elsewhere in Europe --- but as Hoffman explained,
this is not as simple an idea as it sounds.

"Infrastructure would have to be built up in the hosting states, most
notably bunkers," he told Euronews. "I would also say that
forward-deploying French nuclear weapons in Germany wouldn't really make
any difference. If at all, they should be forward-deployed to the
frontline states" --- that is, Russia-facing countries including Poland.

His comments come after Poland said it would like US nuclear warheads to
be deployed on Polish soil.

Out of the shadows

After the end of the Cold War and the demise of the Warsaw Pact, Poland
was rid of the Soviet nuclear weapons that had been stationed on its
territory. Like most European countries, it has since signed up to
various international agreements to limit the spread of nuclear weapons,
including the Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons and the
Comprehensive Nuclear-Test-Ban Treaty.

[Were Poland or another European state to start developing its **own
nuclear weapons**]{.underline}, [it would be abandoning a **norm** that
has held sway in the West for **three decades**]{.underline}.

[The highly developed global nuclear **non-proliferation
regime**]{.underline} [has long been focused on curtailing the **North
Korea**]{.underline}n weapons programme, heading off weapons-grade
enrichment in Iran, [and preventing uncontrolled international flows of
**uranium**]{.underline}, plutonium, and the components needed to enrich
enough of either to create a viable nuclear device.

The prospect of a European state developing weapons itself has not been
bargained for --- but that does not mean an aspiring proliferator would
be able to do so under the radar.

"[None of the European non-nuclear weapon states would likely be able to
**secretly proliferate**]{.underline}," Hoffman said. "Just like
anywhere else in the world, [nuclear infrastructure and nuclear-related
assets in Europe are under the **strict safeguards**]{.underline} [of
the **I**]{.underline}nternational **[A]{.underline}**tomic
**[E]{.underline}**nergy **[A]{.underline}**gency, meaning any
diversions would most likely be noticed."

### No Prolif\-\--1AR

#### Err aff\-\--both the likelihood of proliferation and its impacts are [exaggerated]{.underline}.

Jeffrey H. **Michaels 24**, IEN Senior Fellow in American Foreign Policy

inadvertently come to involve other states, [or that two separate,

matters get out of hand**, especially the inappropriateness of
contemplating nuclear use when the stakes involved have been **limited**
rather than existential]{.underline}.37 Nevertheless, as is so often the
case with the practice of deterrence, the [nuclear scenarios utilized by
political and military actors may be **unrealistic**, but they influence
policy and strategy]{.underline} nonetheless. Talmadge is therefore

Jacklyn **Majnemer &** Patrick **Gill-Tiney 25**, Fellow in Political

[European allies could]{.underline} also [pursue their own nuclear
arsenals]{.underline}. This option would provide sovereign control over

obstacles**]{.underline} to build the capabilities needed for a credible

#### Counterproliferation measures block it.

Liviu **Horovitz &** Claudia **Major 6-17**, Associate in the

Considering the difficulties of extended nuclear deterrence, many
European commentators and decisionmakers instinctively regard the
acquisition of nuclear weapons as the most viable path forward. It is
thus not surprising that, in March 2025, the Polish prime minister
suggested to his country's parliament that Warsaw should keep its
options open. [Most European nations possess the technical expertise and
physical resources necessary to develop their own nuclear arsenals.
However]{.underline}, embarking on [such an undertaking would involve
**significant costs** and **considerable time**]{.underline}. While some
of the world's most advanced industrial nations would most certainly be
able to replicate technologies that are eight decades old, [obtaining
fissile materials, building reliable warheads, and developing effective
delivery systems pose **highly complex challenges**]{.underline}.

And nuclear proliferation is anything but risk-free. [If Europeans
misinterpret U.S. commitments while Washington remains
dedicated]{.underline} to preserving its order, [it is likely to take
steps to **stop its allies**]{.underline}. Past experience suggests that
[the **U**nited **S**tates can mobilize substantial diplomatic,
economic, and political resources to exert pressure on]{.underline} both
[friends and adversaries to prevent nuclear proliferation]{.underline}.
Conversely, if Washington were to fully abandon its commitment to
protecting its allies, [Moscow would]{.underline} likely [see few
reasons to refrain from **using all force at its disposal to prevent
prolif**eration]{.underline}. Considering that [key nuclear facilities
are]{.underline} comparatively [**easy to identify and strike**,
and]{.underline} that [Russia will continue to hold an escalation
edge]{.underline} through its strategic, tactical, and long-range
capabilities, [European nations]{.underline} will likely [face **serious
challenges** in any counterproliferation scenario]{.underline}.

## Impact\-\--Frontline States

### Frontline States\-\--U\-\--2AC

#### Frontline states are already preparing for a world without us.

Carlos **Torralba 25**, Reporter at El País, "Atomic bombs and
landmines: European countries debate their red lines for dealing with
Russia," El País, 04-17-2025, accessed 07-18-2025,
https://english.elpais.com/international/2025-04-17/atomic-bombs-and-landmines-european-countries-debate-their-red-lines-for-dealing-with-russia.html,
DG

While nuclear weapons are being debated behind closed doors in several
European capitals, [all EU members that share a border with Russia are
**accelerating their plans** to withdraw from the Ottawa Treaty, which
prohibits the manufacture, use, stockpiling, and transfer of
anti-personnel mines. Poland, Lithuania, Latvia, and Estonia
announced]{.underline} on March 19 that [they will withdraw]{.underline}
this year. [Finland followed suit]{.underline} two weeks later. On
Tuesday, Karlis Neretnieks, a retired general and director of Sweden's
National Defense College, called on Sweden to also withdraw from the
Ottawa Treaty and acquire anti-personnel mines as soon as possible.

Norway, the only NATO member other than the EU that borders Russia,
maintains its ban on these types of mines and rejects the measure
adopted by its allies. "If we start weakening our commitment, we make it
easier for these weapons to be used again in conflicts around the world,
because we reduce the stigma," declared Barth Eide, the Norwegian
Minister of Defense. Amnesty International and Human Rights Watch have
also harshly criticized the step taken by Poland, Finland, and the
Baltic countries.

Kalev Stoicescu, chairman of the Estonian Parliament's Defense
Committee, justified the controversial measure over the phone: "[If we
have to stop invading hordes, as we see them fighting in Ukraine, we
will be ready to use these]{.underline}, so to speak, inhumane means."
Stoicescu, who was ambassador to the United States and Canada when the
convention was signed in 1997 in Ottawa, maintains that [Estonia wants
to have]{.underline} "[a free hand]{.underline}" [to
confront]{.underline} "the threat posed by [Russia and
Belarus]{.underline}."

Fear of Moscow led Poland, Finland, and the Baltic countries to become
the last EU members to sign the Ottawa Convention. Vilnius, Tallinn, and
Riga did so between 2003 and 2005; Helsinki and Warsaw did not ratify it
until 2012. In addition to initiating the procedure to lift the ban on
anti-personnel mines, Lithuania formally withdrew in March from the
Convention on Cluster Munitions, to which Poland, Finland, Estonia, and
Latvia never acceded.

#### Countries are [actively seeking]{.underline} alternatives.

**Hertling '25** \[Mark; July 16; former commander of U.S. Army Europe;

THE SHAPE OF NATO IS CHANGING. [As the United States **withdraws** from

### Frontline States\-\--Thumpers\-\--2AC

#### Either Trump [thumps]{.underline} OR proves relations are [resilient]{.underline} because countries are [already moving away]{.underline} from the U.S. 

Kristi **Raik 25**, Director of the International Centre for Defence and
Security, Adjunct Professor of International Relations at the University

[When]{.underline} Donald [Trump was re-elected]{.underline} as
